<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the archaeological site. <p><b>Mohenjo-daro</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;h&#39; in &#39;hi&#39;">h</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/dʒ/: &#39;j&#39; in &#39;jam&#39;">dʒ</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a>: <span lang="sd" dir="rtl"><span style="font-size:90%;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1227789315">.mw-parser-output .script-arabic{font-family:"Scheherazade New","SF Arabic",Amiri,"Noto Naskh Arabic","Droid Arabic Naskh","Noto Sans Arabic","Sakkal Majalla","Harmattan","Arabic Typesetting","Arabic Transparent","Times New Roman",Arial,Calibri,"Microsoft Sans Serif","Segoe UI",serif,sans-serif;font-weight:normal}</style><span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">موهن جو دڙو</span>&#8206;</span></span>, <abbr style="font-size:85%" title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#8201;</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span><span class="gloss-text">Mound of the Dead Men</span><span class="gloss-quot">'</span>; <a href="/wiki/Urdu_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Urdu language">Urdu</a>: <span lang="ur" dir="rtl"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" title="Nastaliq" style="font-family: &#39;Jameel Noori Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Urdu Typesetting&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu&#39;, &#39;Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft&#39;, &#39;Hussaini Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq&#39;, IranNastaliq, &#39;Awami Nastaliq&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta3&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta2&#39;, &#39;Awami Nastaliq Beta1&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01&#39;, &#39;Pak Nastaleeq&#39;, &#39;PDMS_Jauhar&#39;, &#39;Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq&#39;; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;">موئن جو دڑو</span></span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="sd-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">&#91;muˑənⁱ<span class="wrap"> </span>dʑoˑ<span class="wrap"> </span>d̪əɽoˑ&#93;</a></span>) is an archaeological site in <a href="/wiki/Larkana_District" title="Larkana District">Larkana District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>. Built <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2500 BCE, it was the largest settlement of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a>, and one of the world's earliest major <a href="/wiki/City" title="City">cities</a>, contemporaneous with the civilizations of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan Crete</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caral%E2%80%93Supe_civilization" title="Caral–Supe civilization">Norte Chico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EB_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EB-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Name_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Name-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>With an estimated population of at least 40,000 people, Mohenjo-daro prospered for several centuries, but by <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1700 BCE had been abandoned,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> along with other large cities of the Indus Valley Civilization.
</p><p>The site was rediscovered in the 1920s. Significant excavation has since been conducted at the site of the city, which was designated a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> in 1980, the first site in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> to be so designated.<sup id="cite_ref-mohenjodaro.net_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohenjodaro.net-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The site is currently threatened by erosion and improper restoration.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCLostMaybe_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCLostMaybe-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Etymology">Etymology</h2></div>
<p>The city's original name is unknown. Based on his analysis of a Mohenjo-daro seal, <a href="/wiki/Iravatham_Mahadevan" title="Iravatham Mahadevan">Iravatham Mahadevan</a> speculates that the city's ancient name could have been <i>Kukkuṭārma</i> ("the city [<i>-rma</i>] of the <a href="/wiki/Cockerel" class="mw-redirect" title="Cockerel">cockerel</a> [<i>kukkuta</i>]").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Cock-fighting" class="mw-redirect" title="Cock-fighting">Cock-fighting</a> may have had ritual and religious significance for the city.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Mohenjo-daro may also have been a <a href="/wiki/Diffusion_of_innovations" title="Diffusion of innovations">point of diffusion</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> of the domesticated chicken found in Africa, Western Asia, Europe and the Americas.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Mohenjo-daro, the modern name for the site, has been interpreted as "Mound of the Dead" in <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Name_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Name-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Location">Location</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:IVC-major-sites-2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/IVC-major-sites-2.jpg/250px-IVC-major-sites-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="307" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/IVC-major-sites-2.jpg/375px-IVC-major-sites-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/IVC-major-sites-2.jpg/500px-IVC-major-sites-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1259" data-file-height="1548" /></a><figcaption>Map showing the major sites and theorised extent of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a>, including the location of the Mohenjo-daro site</figcaption></figure>
<p>Mohenjo-daro is located off the right (west) bank of the lower<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus river</a> in <a href="/wiki/Larkana_District" title="Larkana District">Larkana District</a>, Sindh, Pakistan. It lies on a <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> ridge in  the flood plain of the Indus, around 28 kilometres (17&#160;mi) from the town of <a href="/wiki/Larkana" title="Larkana">Larkana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Historical_context">Historical context</h2></div>
<p>Mohenjo-daro was built in the 26th century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-Ancientindia.co.uk_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ancientindia.co.uk-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It was one of the largest cities of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappan</a> Civilization,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which developed <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3000 BCE from the prehistoric Indus culture. At its height, the Indus Civilization spanned much of what is now Pakistan and North India, extending westwards to the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> border, south to <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> in India and northwards to an outpost in <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, with major urban centers at Harappa, Mohenjo-daro, <a href="/wiki/Lothal" title="Lothal">Lothal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalibangan" title="Kalibangan">Kalibangan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dholavira" title="Dholavira">Dholavira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rakhigarhi" title="Rakhigarhi">Rakhigarhi</a>. Mohenjo-daro was the most advanced city of its time, with remarkably sophisticated civil engineering and urban planning.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When the Indus civilization went into sudden decline <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1900 BCE, Mohenjo-daro was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-Ancientindia.co.uk_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ancientindia.co.uk-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-KenoyerArticle_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KenoyerArticle-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Rediscovery_and_excavation">Rediscovery and excavation</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Ruins_at_Moenjodaro-108221.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Archaeological_Ruins_at_Moenjodaro-108221.jpg/220px-Archaeological_Ruins_at_Moenjodaro-108221.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Archaeological_Ruins_at_Moenjodaro-108221.jpg/330px-Archaeological_Ruins_at_Moenjodaro-108221.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Archaeological_Ruins_at_Moenjodaro-108221.jpg/440px-Archaeological_Ruins_at_Moenjodaro-108221.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption>Archaeological ruins at Moenjodaro</figcaption></figure>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mohenja-daro_excavations_1924.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Mohenja-daro_excavations_1924.jpg/200px-Mohenja-daro_excavations_1924.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="130" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Mohenja-daro_excavations_1924.jpg/300px-Mohenja-daro_excavations_1924.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Mohenja-daro_excavations_1924.jpg/400px-Mohenja-daro_excavations_1924.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3729" data-file-height="2426" /></a><figcaption>Excavations at Mohenjo-daro in 1924</figcaption></figure>
<p>The ruins of the city remained undocumented for around 3,700 years until <a href="/wiki/R._D._Banerji" title="R. D. Banerji">R. D. Banerji</a>, an officer of the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Survey_of_India" title="Archaeological Survey of India">Archaeological Survey of India</a>, visited the site in 1919–20 identifying what he thought to be a <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a> (150–500 CE) known to be there and finding a flint scraper which convinced him of the site's antiquity. This led to large-scale excavations of Mohenjo-daro led by <a href="/wiki/Kashinath_Narayan_Dikshit" title="Kashinath Narayan Dikshit">K. N. Dikshit</a> in 1924–25, and <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_(archaeologist)" title="John Marshall (archaeologist)">John Marshall</a> in 1925–26.<sup id="cite_ref-Possehl_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Possehl-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 1930s major excavations were conducted at the site under the leadership of Marshall, D. K. Dikshitar and <a href="/wiki/Ernest_J._H._Mackay" title="Ernest J. H. Mackay">Ernest Mackay</a>. Further excavations were carried out in 1945 by <a href="/wiki/Mortimer_Wheeler" title="Mortimer Wheeler">Mortimer Wheeler</a> and his trainee, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Hasan_Dani" title="Ahmad Hasan Dani">Ahmad Hasan Dani</a> and <a href="/wiki/F._A._Khan" title="F. A. Khan">F. A. Khan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The last major series of excavations were conducted in 1964 and 1965 by <a href="/wiki/George_F._Dales" title="George F. Dales">George F. Dales</a>. After 1965, excavations were banned due to <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> damage to the exposed structures, and the only projects allowed at the site since have been salvage excavations, surface surveys, and conservation projects. In the 1980s, German and Italian survey groups led by Michael Jansen and Maurizio Tosi used less invasive archeological techniques, such as architectural documentation, surface surveys, and localized probing, to gather further information about Mohenjo-daro.<sup id="cite_ref-mohenjodaro.net_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohenjodaro.net-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A dry core drilling conducted in 2015 by Pakistan's National Fund for Mohenjo-daro revealed that the site is larger than the unearthed area.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Architecture_and_urban_infrastructure">Architecture and urban infrastructure</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sanitation_of_the_Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Sanitation of the Indus Valley Civilisation">Sanitation of the Indus Valley Civilisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harappan_architecture" title="Harappan architecture">Harappan architecture</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Moen_Jo_Daro_(The_Mond_of_the_Deads).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Moen_Jo_Daro_%28The_Mond_of_the_Deads%29.jpg/220px-Moen_Jo_Daro_%28The_Mond_of_the_Deads%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Moen_Jo_Daro_%28The_Mond_of_the_Deads%29.jpg/330px-Moen_Jo_Daro_%28The_Mond_of_the_Deads%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Moen_Jo_Daro_%28The_Mond_of_the_Deads%29.jpg/440px-Moen_Jo_Daro_%28The_Mond_of_the_Deads%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1440" /></a><figcaption>Regularity of streets and buildings suggests the influence of ancient <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> in Mohenjo-daro's construction.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mohenjo-daro.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mohenjo-daro.jpg/220px-Mohenjo-daro.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mohenjo-daro.jpg/330px-Mohenjo-daro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mohenjo-daro.jpg/440px-Mohenjo-daro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a><figcaption>View of the site's <a href="/wiki/Great_Bath,_Mohenjo-daro" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Bath, Mohenjo-daro">Great Bath</a>, showing the surrounding urban layout</figcaption></figure>
<p>Mohenjo-daro has a <a href="/wiki/Planned_city" class="mw-redirect" title="Planned city">planned layout</a> with <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rectilinear" class="extiw" title="wikt:rectilinear">rectilinear</a> buildings arranged on a <a href="/wiki/Grid_plan" title="Grid plan">grid plan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most were built of fired and mortared <a href="/wiki/Brick" title="Brick">brick</a>; some incorporated sun-dried <a href="/wiki/Mud-brick" class="mw-redirect" title="Mud-brick">mud-brick</a> and wooden superstructures. The covered area of Mohenjo-daro is estimated at 300 <a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">hectares</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i>Oxford Handbook of Cities in World History</i> offers a "weak" estimate of a peak population of around 40,000.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The sheer size of the city, and its provision of public buildings and facilities, suggests a high level of social organization.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh389_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh389-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The city is divided into two parts, the so-called Citadel and the Lower City. The Citadel – a mud-brick mound around 12 metres (39&#160;ft) high – is known to have supported public baths, a large residential structure designed to house about 5,000 citizens, and two large assembly halls. The city had a central marketplace, with a large central well. Individual households or groups of households obtained their water from smaller wells. Waste water was channeled to covered drains that lined the major streets. Some houses, presumably those of more prestigious  inhabitants, include rooms that appear to have been set aside for bathing, and one building had an underground furnace (known as a <a href="/wiki/Hypocaust" title="Hypocaust">hypocaust</a>), possibly for heated bathing. Most houses had inner courtyards, with doors that opened onto side-lanes. Some buildings had two stories. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Major_buildings">Major buildings</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_bath_view_Mohenjodaro.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Great_bath_view_Mohenjodaro.JPG/220px-Great_bath_view_Mohenjodaro.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Great_bath_view_Mohenjodaro.JPG/330px-Great_bath_view_Mohenjodaro.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Great_bath_view_Mohenjodaro.JPG/440px-Great_bath_view_Mohenjodaro.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a><figcaption>The Great Bath</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1950, Sir Mortimer Wheeler identified one large building in Mohenjo-daro as a "Great Granary". Certain wall-divisions in its massive wooden superstructure appeared to be grain storage-bays, complete with air-ducts to dry the grain. According to Wheeler, carts would have brought grain from the countryside and unloaded them directly into the bays. However, <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Mark_Kenoyer" title="Jonathan Mark Kenoyer">Jonathan Mark Kenoyer</a> noted the complete lack of evidence for grain at the "granary", which, he argued, might therefore be better termed a "Great Hall" of uncertain function.<sup id="cite_ref-KenoyerArticle_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KenoyerArticle-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Close to the "Great Granary" is a large and elaborate public bath, sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/Great_Bath" title="Great Bath">Great Bath</a>. From a colonnaded courtyard, steps lead down to the brick-built pool, which was waterproofed by a lining of <a href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a>. The pool measures 12 metres (39&#160;ft) long, 7 metres (23&#160;ft) wide and 2.4 metres (7.9&#160;ft) deep. It may have been used for religious purification. Other large buildings include a "Pillared Hall", thought to be an assembly hall of some kind, and the so-called "College Hall", a complex of buildings comprising 78 rooms, thought to have been a priestly residence. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Fortifications">Fortifications</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Other_side_of_Moenjodaro_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Other_side_of_Moenjodaro_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg/220px-Other_side_of_Moenjodaro_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Other_side_of_Moenjodaro_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg/330px-Other_side_of_Moenjodaro_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Other_side_of_Moenjodaro_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg/440px-Other_side_of_Moenjodaro_by_Usman_Ghani.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a><figcaption>Excavation of the city revealed very tall <a href="/wiki/Water_well" class="mw-redirect" title="Water well">wells</a> (left), which it seems were continually built up as flooding and rebuilding raised the elevation of street level.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Mohenjo-daro had no series of city walls, but was fortified with guard towers to the west of the main settlement, and defensive fortifications to the south. Considering these fortifications and the structure of other major <a href="/wiki/Indus_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Indus valley">Indus valley</a> cities like <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a>, it is postulated that Mohenjo-daro was an administrative center. Both Harappa and Mohenjo-daro share relatively the same architectural layout, and were generally not heavily fortified like other Indus Valley sites. It is obvious from the identical city layouts of all Indus sites that there was some kind of political or administrative centrality, but the extent and functioning of an administrative center remains unclear.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Water_supply_and_wells">Water supply and wells</h3></div>
<p>The location of Mohenjo-daro was built in a relatively short period of time, with the water supply system and wells being some of the first planned constructions.
<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the excavations done so far, over 700 wells are present at Mohenjo-daro, alongside drainage and bathing systems.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This number is unheard of when compared to other civilizations at the time, such as Egypt or Mesopotamia, and the quantity of wells transcribes as one well for every three houses.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of the large number of wells, it is believed that the inhabitants relied solely on annual rainfall, as well as the Indus River's course remaining close to the site, alongside the wells providing water for long periods of time in the case of the city coming under siege.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Due to the period in which these wells were built and used, it is likely that the circular brick well design used at this and many other Harappan sites are an invention that should be credited to the Indus civilization, as there is no existing evidence of this design from Mesopotamia or Egypt at this time, and even later.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sewage and waste water for buildings at the site were disposed of via a centralized drainage system that ran alongside the site's streets.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These drains that ran alongside the road were effective at allowing most human waste and sewage to be disposed of as the drains most likely took the waste toward the Indus River.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Flooding_and_rebuilding">Flooding and rebuilding</h3></div>
<p>The city also had large platforms perhaps intended as defense against flooding.<sup id="cite_ref-McIntosh389_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh389-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to a theory first advanced by Wheeler, the city could have been flooded and silted over, perhaps six times, and later rebuilt in the same location.<sup id="cite_ref-Dales_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dales-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For some archaeologists, it was believed that a final flood that helped engulf the city in a sea of mud brought about the abandonment of the site.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Possehl" title="Gregory Possehl">Gregory Possehl</a> was the first to theorize that the floods were caused by overuse and expansion upon the land, and that the mud flood was not the reason the site was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Instead of a mud flood wiping part of the city out in one fell swoop, Possehl coined the possibility of constant mini-floods throughout the year, paired with the land being worn out by crops, pastures, and resources for bricks and pottery spelled the downfall of the site.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notable_artefacts">Notable artefacts</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Disha_Kaka_Boat_with_Direction_Finding_Birds,_model_of_Mohenjo-Daro_seal,_3000_BCE.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Disha_Kaka_Boat_with_Direction_Finding_Birds%2C_model_of_Mohenjo-Daro_seal%2C_3000_BCE.jpg/330px-Disha_Kaka_Boat_with_Direction_Finding_Birds%2C_model_of_Mohenjo-Daro_seal%2C_3000_BCE.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="176" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Disha_Kaka_Boat_with_Direction_Finding_Birds%2C_model_of_Mohenjo-Daro_seal%2C_3000_BCE.jpg/495px-Disha_Kaka_Boat_with_Direction_Finding_Birds%2C_model_of_Mohenjo-Daro_seal%2C_3000_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Disha_Kaka_Boat_with_Direction_Finding_Birds%2C_model_of_Mohenjo-Daro_seal%2C_3000_BCE.jpg/660px-Disha_Kaka_Boat_with_Direction_Finding_Birds%2C_model_of_Mohenjo-Daro_seal%2C_3000_BCE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3359" data-file-height="1788" /></a><figcaption>Boat with direction finding birds to find land.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Model of Mohenjo-Daro seal, 2500-1750 BCE.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Numerous objects found in excavation include seated and standing figures, copper and stone tools, carved <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">balance-scales and weights</a>, gold and <a href="/wiki/Jasper" title="Jasper">jasper</a> jewellery, and children's toys.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many bronze and copper pieces, such as figurines and bowls, have been recovered from the site, showing that the inhabitants of Mohenjo-daro understood how to utilize the <a href="/wiki/Lost_wax_technique" class="mw-redirect" title="Lost wax technique">lost wax technique</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The furnaces found at the site are believed to have been used for copperworks and melting the metals as opposed to smelting. There even seems to be an entire section of the city dedicated to shell-working, located in the northeastern part of the site.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some of the most prominent copperworks recovered from the site are the copper tablets which have examples of the untranslated <a href="/wiki/Indus_script" title="Indus script">Indus script</a> and iconography.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While the script has not been deciphered yet, many of the images on the tablets match another tablet and both hold the same caption in the Indus language, with the example given showing three tablets with the image of a mountain goat and the inscription on the back reading the same letters for the three tablets.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Pottery and terracotta <a href="/wiki/Sherd" class="mw-redirect" title="Sherd">sherds</a> have been recovered from the site, with many of the pots having deposits of ash in them, leading archeologists to believe they were either used to hold the ashes of a person or as a way to warm up a home located in the site.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These heaters, or braziers, were ways to heat the house while also being able to be utilized in a manner of cooking or straining, while others solely believe they were used for heating.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The finds from Mohenjo-daro were initially deposited in the <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Museum" title="Lahore Museum">Lahore Museum</a>, but later moved to the ASI headquarters at New Delhi, where a new "Central Imperial Museum" was being planned for the new capital of the British Raj, in which at least a selection would be displayed.  It became apparent that Indian independence was approaching, but the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">Partition of India</a> was not anticipated until late in the process.  The new Pakistani authorities requested the return of the Harappan pieces excavated on their territory, but the Indian authorities refused.  Eventually an agreement was reached, whereby the finds, totalling some 12,000 objects (most <a href="/wiki/Sherd" class="mw-redirect" title="Sherd">sherds</a> of pottery), were split equally between the countries; in some cases this was taken very literally, with some necklaces and girdles having their beads separated into two piles.  In the case of the "two most celebrated sculpted figures", Pakistan asked for and received the <a href="/wiki/Priest-king_(sculpture)" class="mw-redirect" title="Priest-king (sculpture)"><i>Priest-king</i></a>, while India retained the much smaller <a href="/wiki/Dancing_Girl_(prehistoric_sculpture)" title="Dancing Girl (prehistoric sculpture)"><i>Dancing Girl</i></a>,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and also the <i><a href="/wiki/Pashupati_seal" title="Pashupati seal">Pashupati seal</a></i>.
</p><p>Most of the objects from Mohenjo-daro retained by India are in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_India" title="National Museum of India">National Museum of India</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a> and those returned to Pakistan in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Pakistan" title="National Museum of Pakistan">National Museum of Pakistan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>, with many also in the museum now established at Mohenjo-daro itself. In 1939, a small representative group of artefacts excavated at the site was transferred to the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> by the Director-General of the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Survey_of_India" title="Archaeological Survey of India">Archaeological Survey of India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mother_Goddess_Idol">Mother Goddess Idol</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Picture_of_original_Godess.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Picture_of_original_Godess.jpg/170px-Picture_of_original_Godess.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="209" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Picture_of_original_Godess.jpg/255px-Picture_of_original_Godess.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Picture_of_original_Godess.jpg/340px-Picture_of_original_Godess.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1452" data-file-height="1788" /></a><figcaption>"The Mother Goddess"</figcaption></figure>
<p>Discovered by <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_(archaeologist)" title="John Marshall (archaeologist)">John Marshall</a> in 1931, the <a href="/wiki/Cult_image" title="Cult image">idol</a> appears to mimic certain characteristics that match the <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">Mother Goddess</a> belief common in many early Near East civilizations.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sculptures and figurines depicting women have been observed as part of Harappan culture and religion, as multiple female pieces were recovered from Marshall's archaeological digs.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These figures were not categorized correctly, according to Marshall, meaning that where they were recovered from the site is not actually clear. One of said figures is 18.7&#160;cm tall and is currently on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Pakistan" title="National Museum of Pakistan">National Museum of Pakistan</a>, in Karachi.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The fertility and motherhood aspects on display on the idols is represented by the female genitalia that is presented in an almost exaggerated style as stated by Marshall, with him inferring that such figurines are offerings to the goddess, as opposed to the typical understanding of them being idols representing the goddess's likeness.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of the figurines being unique in terms of hairstyles, body proportions, as well as headdresses and jewelry, there are theories as to who these figurines actually represent.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shereen_Ratnagar" title="Shereen Ratnagar">Shereen Ratnagar</a> theorizes that because of their uniqueness and dispersed discovery throughout the site that they could be figurines of ordinary household women, who commissioned these pieces to be used in rituals or healing ceremonies to help aforementioned individual women.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_42-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dancing_Girl">Dancing Girl</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dancing_Girl_(prehistoric_sculpture)" title="Dancing Girl (prehistoric sculpture)">Dancing Girl (prehistoric sculpture)</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Dancing_Girl,_in_a_photogravure_by_Alfred_Nawrath,1938.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_Dancing_Girl%2C_in_a_photogravure_by_Alfred_Nawrath%2C1938.jpg/170px-The_Dancing_Girl%2C_in_a_photogravure_by_Alfred_Nawrath%2C1938.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="257" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_Dancing_Girl%2C_in_a_photogravure_by_Alfred_Nawrath%2C1938.jpg/255px-The_Dancing_Girl%2C_in_a_photogravure_by_Alfred_Nawrath%2C1938.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/The_Dancing_Girl%2C_in_a_photogravure_by_Alfred_Nawrath%2C1938.jpg/340px-The_Dancing_Girl%2C_in_a_photogravure_by_Alfred_Nawrath%2C1938.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="1133" /></a><figcaption>"<a href="/wiki/Dancing_Girl_(Mohenjo-daro)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dancing Girl (Mohenjo-daro)">The Dancing Girl</a>" </figcaption></figure><p>
A <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> statuette dubbed the "Dancing Girl", 10.5 centimetres (4.1&#160;in) high<sup id="cite_ref-national_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-national-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and about 4,500 years old, was found in 'HR area' of Mohenjo-daro in 1926; it is now in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum,_New_Delhi" class="mw-redirect" title="National Museum, New Delhi">National Museum, New Delhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-national_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-national-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1973, British archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Mortimer_Wheeler" title="Mortimer Wheeler">Mortimer Wheeler</a> described the item as his favorite statuette:</p><blockquote><p>She's about fifteen years old I should think, not more, but she stands there with bangles all the way up her arm and nothing else on. A girl perfectly, for the moment, perfectly confident of herself and the world. There's nothing like her, I think, in the world.</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_(archaeologist)" title="John Marshall (archaeologist)">John Marshall</a>, another archeologist at Mohenjo-daro, described the figure as "a young girl, her hand on her hip in a half-impudent posture, and legs slightly forward as she beats time to the music with her legs and feet."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Possehl" title="Gregory Possehl">Gregory Possehl</a> said of the statuette, "We may not be certain that she was a dancer, but she was good at what she did and she knew it". The statue led to two important discoveries about the civilization: first, that they knew metal blending, casting and other sophisticated methods of working with ore, and secondly that entertainment, especially dance, was part of the culture.<sup id="cite_ref-national_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-national-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Priest-King">Priest-King</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Priest-King_(sculpture)" title="Priest-King (sculpture)">Priest-King (sculpture)</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mohenjo-daro_Priesterk%C3%B6nig.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mohenjo-daro_Priesterk%C3%B6nig.jpeg/170px-Mohenjo-daro_Priesterk%C3%B6nig.jpeg" decoding="async" width="170" height="219" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mohenjo-daro_Priesterk%C3%B6nig.jpeg/255px-Mohenjo-daro_Priesterk%C3%B6nig.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mohenjo-daro_Priesterk%C3%B6nig.jpeg/340px-Mohenjo-daro_Priesterk%C3%B6nig.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="584" data-file-height="754" /></a><figcaption>"The Priest-King", a seated stone sculpture at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Pakistan" title="National Museum of Pakistan">National Museum</a>, Karachi</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1927, a seated male <a href="/wiki/Soapstone" title="Soapstone">soapstone</a> figure was found in a building with unusually ornamental brickwork and a wall-niche. Though there is no evidence that <a href="/wiki/Priests" class="mw-redirect" title="Priests">priests</a> or <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarchs</a> ruled Mohenjo-daro, archaeologists dubbed this dignified figure a "Priest-King". The sculpture is 17.5 centimetres (6.9&#160;in) tall, and shows a neatly bearded man with pierced earlobes and a <a href="/wiki/Fillet_(clothing)" title="Fillet (clothing)">fillet</a> around his head, possibly all that is left of a once-elaborate hairstyle or head-dress; his hair is combed back. He wears an armband, and a cloak with drilled <a href="/wiki/Trefoil" title="Trefoil">trefoil</a>, single circle and double circle motifs, which show traces of red. His eyes might have originally been inlaid.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pashupati_seal">Pashupati seal</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pashupati_seal" title="Pashupati seal">Pashupati seal</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Shiva_Pashupati.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Shiva_Pashupati.jpg/170px-Shiva_Pashupati.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="171" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Shiva_Pashupati.jpg/255px-Shiva_Pashupati.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Shiva_Pashupati.jpg/340px-Shiva_Pashupati.jpg 2x" data-file-width="920" data-file-height="926" /></a><figcaption>The <i><a href="/wiki/Pashupati_seal" title="Pashupati seal">Pashupati seal</a></i></figcaption></figure>
<p>A seal discovered at the site bears the image of a seated, cross-legged and possibly <a href="/wiki/Phallus" title="Phallus">ithyphallic</a> figure surrounded by animals. The figure has been interpreted by some scholars as a <a href="/wiki/Yogi" title="Yogi">yogi</a>, and by others as a three-headed "proto-<a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>" as "Lord of Animals".
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Seven-stranded_necklace">Seven-stranded necklace</h3></div>
<p>Sir Mortimer Wheeler was especially fascinated with this artifact, which he believed to be at least 4,500 years old. The necklace has an S-shaped clasp with seven strands, each over 4&#160;ft long, of bronze-metal bead-like nuggets which connect each arm of the "S" in <a href="/wiki/Filigree" title="Filigree">filigree</a>. Each strand has between 220 and 230 of the many-faceted nuggets, and there are about 1,600 nuggets in total. The necklace weighs about 250 grams in total, and is presently held in a private collection in India.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_Mohenjo-daro_ruler">The Mohenjo-daro ruler</h3></div>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Ruler"></span>
The Indus Valley civilization employed <a href="/wiki/Ruler" title="Ruler">rulers</a> made of <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> for measuring length circa 1500&#160;BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelaw14_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelaw14-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Mohenjo-Daro ruler is divided into units corresponding to 34 millimetres (1.32&#160;in) and these are further marked in decimal subdivisions with great accuracy, to within 0.13&#160;mm (0.005&#160;in). A ruler found at <a href="/wiki/Lothal" title="Lothal">Lothal</a> (2400&#160;BCE) is calibrated to about <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>1.6&#160;mm (<span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">16</span></span>&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelaw14_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelaw14-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The decimal subdivision on the ruler is noteworthy, as it predates the modern metric system by 3000 years or more. The base unit used was known as the <i>angula</i> (finger) and is about 17&#160;mm. A piece of shell found in Mackay's excavation is very precisely incised with lines spaced 6.7&#160;mm (0.264&#160;in) apart. Ancient bricks found throughout the region have dimensions that correspond to these units.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Conservation_and_current_state">Conservation and current state</h2></div>
<p>An initial agreement to fund restoration was agreed through the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Educational,_Scientific,_and_Cultural_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Educational, Scientific, and Cultural Organization">United Nations Educational, Scientific, and Cultural Organization</a> (UNESCO) in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> on 27 May 1980.  Contributions were made by a number of other countries to the project:
</p>

<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ghanghro_location_-_Mohenjo-daro.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ghanghro_location_-_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/220px-Ghanghro_location_-_Mohenjo-daro.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Ghanghro_location_-_Mohenjo-daro.jpg/330px-Ghanghro_location_-_Mohenjo-daro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Ghanghro_location_-_Mohenjo-daro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="298" /></a><figcaption>Surviving structures at Mohenjo-daro, 2005</figcaption></figure>
<p>Preservation work for Mohenjo-daro was suspended in December 1996 after funding from the Pakistani government and international organizations stopped. Site conservation work resumed in April 1997, using funds made available by the UNESCO. The 20-year funding plan provided $10 million to protect the site and standing structures from <a href="/wiki/Flooding" class="mw-redirect" title="Flooding">flooding</a>. In 2011, responsibility for the preservation of the site was transferred to the government of Sindh.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Currently the site is threatened by groundwater <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> and improper restoration. Many walls have already collapsed, while others are crumbling from the ground up. In 2012, Pakistani archaeologists warned that, without improved conservation measures, the site could disappear by 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCLostMaybe_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCLostMaybe-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="2014_Sindh_Festival">2014 Sindh Festival</h3></div>
<p>The Mohenjo-daro site was further threatened in January 2014, when <a href="/wiki/Bilawal_Bhutto_Zardari" title="Bilawal Bhutto Zardari">Bilawal Bhutto Zardari</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_People%27s_Party" title="Pakistan People&#39;s Party">Pakistan People's Party</a> chose the site for Sindh Festival's inauguration ceremony. This would have exposed the site to mechanical operations, including excavation and drilling. Farzand Masih, head of the Department of Archaeology at <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Punjab" title="University of the Punjab">Punjab University</a> warned that such activity was banned under the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Antiquity_Act&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Antiquity Act (page does not exist)">Antiquity Act</a>, saying "You cannot even hammer a nail at an archaeological site."  On 31 January 2014, a case was filed in the <a href="/wiki/Sindh_High_Court" title="Sindh High Court">Sindh High Court</a> to bar the Sindh government from continuing with the event.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The festival was held by PPP at the historic site, despite all the protest by both national and international historians and educators.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Climate">Climate</h2></div>
<p>Mohenjo-daro has a <a href="/wiki/Hot_desert_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Hot desert climate">hot desert climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>BWh</i>) with extremely hot summers and mild winters. The highest recorded temperature is 53.7&#160;°C (128.7&#160;°F), recorded in May 2010 and the lowest recorded temperature is −5.4&#160;°C (22.3&#160;°F), recorded in January 2006. Rainfall is low, and mainly occurs in the monsoon season (July–September). The average annual rainfall of Mohenjo-daro is 100.1&#160;mm and mainly occurs in the monsoon season. The highest annual rainfall ever is 1023.8&#160;mm, recorded in 2022, and the lowest annual rainfall ever is 10&#160;mm, recorded in 1987.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Peter Clark (editor), <i>The Oxford Handbook of Cities in World History</i>, Oxford University Press, 2013, pp. 158–59; "since it is impossible to ascertain what proportion of the city was used for habitation the basis for this [population] estimate is weak." For lower area estimate of 85 hectares, see note 25, citing U. Singh, <i>A History of Ancient and Medieval India,</i> Delhi, Pearson Education, 2008, p. 149. See also FR Alchin and G Erdosy, The Archaeology of Early Historic Asia: The Emergence of Cities and States, Cambridge University Press, 1995, p. 57.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-McIntosh389-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-McIntosh389_21-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-McIntosh389_21-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">McIntosh (2008), p. 389. "The enormous amount of labor involved in the creation of Mohenjo-daro's flood defense platforms (calculated at around 4 million man-days) indicates the existence of an authority able to plan the construction and to mobilize and feed the requisite labor force."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">McIntosh (2008), p. 118. "More than seven hundred wells were sunk at Mohenjo-daro when the city was built. Over the centuries houses were rebuilt and street levels rose; new courses of bricks were therefore added to the wells to keep their tops at the same height with respect to the street. The removal of earth and debris during the excavation of the city has left many wells standing like towers high above the exposed remnants of earlier streets."</span>
</li>
